He might never have matched the feat during an isolated go-round, but here are his per-game numbers for the &lt;a href=\"http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/r/roberos01.html#1961-1966-sum:per_game\"&gt;first six seasons&lt;/a&gt; of his career combined:&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;30.4 points. 10.0 rebounds. 10.7 assists.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;That's a triple-double right there.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Robertson's all-around excellence allowed him to earn quite a few win shares, as you might expect. In addition to a studly 23.2 PER, he accumulated 189.2 win shares over the course of his career, more than any point guard other than John Stockton. They piled up at a rate of 0.207 per 48 minutes, faster than all but four floor generals throughout NBA history.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;But it's in the performance metrics where Robertson stands out, even if his Advancement Shares aren't too special and are boosted by Kareem Abdul-Jabbar's presence when they joined forces on the Milwaukee Bucks.&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Robertson's 31.25 Career Contributions per season are not only the top mark at the position, but they blow everyone else out. Chris Paul's 28.6 are the No. 2 average, and only the next player to appear in this countdown joins them in the above-25 club. Just six players at any position top Robertson here, in fact.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;He's also one of only three point guards to win LMVP, his 2.479 MVP Shares trail only the top-ranked player at the position and his 4.2 LMVP Shares place him behind just seven players in NBA history, none of whom are point guards.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;hr&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Metric Cheat Sheet&lt;/strong&gt;&nbsp;(refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)&nbsp;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)&lt;/em&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;","url":"http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/026/59fe531988a67e982a422b8373e86f62_crop_exact.jpg?w=340&amp;h=234&amp;q=85","embed_code":null,"id":3798026,"updatedAt":"2015-03-18T22:29:30-04:00","title":"3. lists the player at more frequently throughout the course of his career.&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;While this might be surprising, given that West is typically found in rankings of the league's premier 2-guards, he was listed as a point guard during 12 of his career's 14 seasons.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;So, point guard it is.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;In 1972,&nbsp;&lt;em&gt;Sports Illustrated&nbsp;&lt;/em&gt;ran a piece with the following quote, as Bill Simmons relayed in&nbsp;&lt;em&gt;The Book of Basketba&lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;ll&lt;/em&gt;: \"There has been a groundswell for West the last few seasons, so that now he is often accepted as the equal, or the superior, of Oscar Robertson as the finest guard of all time.\"&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Obviously, players have come around and dethroned those two, but it was a valid sentiment at the time. Plus, West only built his resume with two more deserving All-Star seasons, one of which saw him named First Team All-NBA. Meanwhile, Robertson quickly declined during the years just following that &lt;em&gt;Sports Illustrated&lt;/em&gt; claim.&nbsp;&lt;em&gt;&lt;br&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Throughout their careers as a whole, the two point guards had fairly similar resumes. Robertson was a slightly better offensive player in a vacuum, and he was certainly more valuable to his teams, given West's superstar teammates. But the man who would go on to be the model for the NBA's logo was an unbelievably gifted defender, which is part of what pushes him over the top.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;So too does his playoff success, as despite only winning a single ring, West consistently put his team in a position to take home the trophy. Even without too many titles working in his favor, he finishes No. 2 and No. 6 in Playoff Performance and Advancement Shares respectively among point guards.&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;hr&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Metric Cheat Sheet&lt;/strong&gt;&nbsp;(refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)&nbsp;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)&lt;/em&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;","url":"http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/027/91389fdf38670a2cc45038eae40eb99e_crop_exact.jpg?w=340&amp;h=234&amp;q=85","embed_code":null,"id":3798027,"updatedAt":"2015-03-23T11:40:39-04:00","title":"2. LMVPs, 1.2 LMVP Shares&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;The only flaw on Magic Johnson's resume is the lack of an LMVP, which is rather hard to earn when you're consistently playing on teams that are right near the top of the NBA standings year in and year out.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Other than that, he has it all covered.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Just take a peek at all the categories in which he leads the field of point guards: Playoff Performance, Advancement Shares, assists per game, true shooting percentage (even without a potent perimeter jumper), total rebounding percentage, MVPs and MVP shares.&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Beyond that, he finishes in the top five for Career Contributions per season, titles, rebounds per game, blocks per game, PER, offensive rating, win shares, win shares per 48 minutes, All-Star selections and All-NBA honors.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;But perhaps most impressively, he put together this type of legacy despite having his career cut short.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Johnson missed four seasons in his early 30s as he dealt with HIV. And though he was still a valuable player when he returned to the Los Angeles Lakers for the 1995-96 campaign, he clearly wasn't the legendary floor general he'd previously been.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;Whether we're talking about scoring, distributing, rebounding, providing value to his teams, playing efficient basketball, making crowds believe in, well, Magic, winning playoff games or doing whatever it took to win, this particular 1-guard was the best in the business.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;hr&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Metric Cheat Sheet&lt;/strong&gt;&nbsp;(refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)&nbsp;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)&lt;/em&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;","url":"http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/028/60de377a10943230e781f318282960f6_crop_exact.jpg?w=340&amp;h=234&amp;q=85","embed_code":null,"id":3798028,"updatedAt":"2015-03-19T11:44:47-04:00","title":"1.          <h1 class="article_title" itemprop="headline name">
          B/R NBA Legends 100: Ranking the Greatest Point Guards of All Time
         </h1>
         <h4 class="article_author" itemprop="author">
          <address class="article_author-info" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
           By
           <a class="article_author-link" href="/users/331034-adam-fromal" itemprop="url" title="Adam Fromal">
            <span itemprop="name">
             Adam  Fromal
            </span>
           </a>
           ,
           <span class="author-rank">
            National NBA Featured Columnist
           </span>
          </address>
          <time class="article_timestamp" data-updated_at="1427108401" itemprop="datePublished">
           Mar 23, 2015
          </time>
         </h4>
         <div id="top-share-buttons-region">
          <ul class="share-buttons cf share-buttons--top">

        <div class="article_pages ">
         <section class="article_page js-article-page-1 cf is-active " data-page="1">
          <header class="article_page-header">
           <div class="article_nav-bar br-texture--sudo br-texture--sudo-fade-to-white">
            <div class="paginators paginators--top">
             <a class="paginator paginator--secondary paginator--prev" href="javascript: void(0)" title="Previous Page">

             </a>
             <div class="paginators_counter">

              <span class="br-light-grey">
               of
              </span>

              </span>
             </div>
             <a class="paginator paginator--secondary paginator--next" href="javascript: void(0)" title="Next Page">

            <span class="bar-headline">

            </span>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="B/R NBA Legends 100: Ranking the Greatest Point Guards of All Time" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/001/hi-res-3206ef012ebbc95ce4cd8710e58f7585_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Andrew D. Bernstein/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_info team-color-border cf">
           <div class="total-reads article-stats">
            <div class="team-color-text article-stats_count">
             319.2K
            </div>
            <div class="article-stats_label">
             Reads
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="article_comments js-open-comments article-stats ">
            <div class="comments-count team-color-text article-stats_count">
             984
            </div>
            <div class="comments-label article-stats_label">
             Comments
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <em>
             Editor's note: This is the first installment of Bleacher Report's series ranking the greatest
             <a href="http://bleacherreport.com/nba">
              NBA
             </a>
             players of all-time at every position, culminating in a top 100 players of all time ranking next week.
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            Throughout NBA history, no position has been more loaded with talent than point guard.
            <strong>
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            It started with the hegemony of Bob Cousy and Oscar Robertson, who ran the show before giving way to basketball geniuses like Walt Frazier and Lenny Wilkens. John Stockton and Magic Johnson took the baton in through the '80s and '90s, and now, we're in the midst of a golden era of point guards.
           </p>
           <p>
            Even though the best floor generals of the 2000s are largely gone or playing out the twilights of their careers, the position is in great hands with guys like
            <a href="http://bleacherreport.com/chris-paul">
             Chris Paul
            </a>
            , Rajon Rondo and the many up-and-comers.
           </p>
           <p>
            But how does everyone stack up throughout NBA history?
           </p>
           <p>
            This series is about more than those few standouts who still suit up in the Association. We're interested in how the legends of the point guard position compare to one another.
           </p>
           <p>
            We're not just concerned with the best peaks. It's not about the longest careers. Instead, we're interested in the strength of an entire career evaluated largely through numbers. However, the prominence of metrics and advanced statistics doesn't mean context can be thrown out the window.
           </p>
           <p>
            Everything matters.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             Note: All stats come from
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/">
              Basketball-Reference.com
             </a>
             and are current through March 21, unless otherwise indicated. That's also the source of positional decisions throughout this series of articles. Whichever position a player is listed at for the majority of the seasons in his career is where he'll be placed in the series.
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
          <a class="paginator paginator--secondary paginator--next" href="javascript: void(0)" title="Begin Slideshow">
           Begin Slideshow
           <span class="aquo">
            
           </span>
          </a>
         </section>
         <section class="article_page article_page--secondary js-article-page-2 cf display-none " data-page="2">
          <header class="article_page-header">
           <div class="article_nav-bar br-texture--sudo br-texture--sudo-fade-to-white">
            <div class="paginators paginators--top">
             <a class="paginator paginator--secondary paginator--prev" href="javascript: void(0)" title="Previous Page">
              <span class="aquo">
               
              </span>
              Prev
             </a>
             <div class="paginators_counter">
              2
              <span class="br-light-grey">
               of
              </span>
              <span class="paginators_page-count">
               29
              </span>
             </div>
             <a class="paginator paginator--secondary paginator--next" href="javascript: void(0)" title="Next Page">
              Next
              <span class="aquo">
               
              </span>
             </a>
            </div>
            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             Important: Glossary of New Metrics That Factor into Evaluation
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="Important: Glossary of New Metrics That Factor into Evaluation" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/002/hi-res-1bb57c09f56f0edee908e15048a8a04d_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Nathaniel S. Butler/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            Though this article will rely heavily on established advanced metrics like win shares, player efficiency rating, true shooting percentage and more, I've also developed a new set of performance metrics that can be used to compare players across eras.
           </p>
           <p>
            As you'll soon see, these metrics will be displayed for every featured player, and they'll be discussed quite often throughout the descriptions of the players in question. Therefore, it's best to familiarize yourself with them now.
           </p>
           <p>
            Understanding the exact calculations is unnecessary for these purposes, but do take the time to understand the principles and purposes, as that will allow you to fully grasp the justifications for the order of this countdown.
           </p>
           <p>
            With traditional metrics, we can gauge how well a player performed during the regular season throughout his career. But with these new ones, we have insight into his playoff performances as well as how valuable he was to his team and throughout the league in general.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Playoff Performance (PP)
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            Derived by multiplying
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/about/glossary.html">
             game score
            </a>
            by the number of playoff appearances, this simply shows the strength of a player's statistical production during the postseason. It rewards both quality of play and longevity, as the top scores are only achieved by maintaining excellent performances over the course of multiple deep playoff runs.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Advancement Share (AS)
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            This shows how deep a player advanced into the playoffs. Different rounds are weighted differently250 possible points for a title, 100 for an unsuccessful appearance in the NBA Finals and 50 for a conference finals exitbut not every player earns all of the possible points.
           </p>
           <p>
            To recognize that some players are bigger contributors than others, the advancement scores are weighted by how much time a player spends on the court. Someone who wins a title but only plays 20 minutes per game will receive a lower percentage of the possible points than a teammate who played 35 minutes per contest.
           </p>
           <p>
            As a result, this shows both playoff success and relative importance during the run of the player in question.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Contributions (CC)
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            Win shares are supposed to be an approximation of how many wins a player provided to his team during a given season, so dividing win shares by team wins should give an estimate of the percentage of value that player was responsible for.Multiplying that by how successful a team was that year (based on TeamRtng+, a combination of
            <a href="http://bleacherreport.com/articles/2185159-ranking-the-nbas-20-best-defenses-of-all-time">
             DRtng+
            </a>
            and
            <a href="http://bleacherreport.com/articles/2185102-ranking-the-nbas-20-best-offenses-of-all-time">
             ORtng+
            </a>
            ) accounts for both a player's value and the strength of the team he was contributing to.
           </p>
           <p>
            Career Contributions sums a player's scores for every season of his career, showing how much value he provided during his NBA life.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Contributions per Season (CC/Season)
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            This shows the number of Career Contributions that a player earned during an average season. It's no more complicated than that.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Literal MVPs (LMVPs)
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            MVP literally stands for "Most Valuable Player," though the award is usually given to the best player on one of the best teams, depending on the narrative, the glamorous play of the candidate and other factors. A Literal MVP, or LMVP, is given instead to the player with the top Career Contributions value during the season in question.
           </p>
           <p>
            An LMVP can go to a player on the best team in the league, but it can also be handed to a player who was essentially a one-man wrecking crew on one of the bottom-feeders. The strength of the team doesn't matter save for the Career Contributions calculation.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Literal MVP Shares (LMVP Shares)
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            Rather than only rewarding the LMVP, we're giving credit to every player who was the top contributor for his team during a given season. Team-leading win-share producers were sorted by Career Contributions before being handed LMVP shares according to their finish on that leaderboard.
           </p>
           <p>
            The LMVP himself gets a full LMVP share. Second place receives 0.5 LMVP shares. Third place gets 0.33 LMVP shares and so on and so forth.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            This appears in the information of each slide, and it's a method of representing a player's peak. Rather than arbitrarily selecting his best season, we're meshing together the best performances of his career for each per-game stat.
           </p>
           <p>
            That means his points per game could come from his rookie year, while his rebounds per game could be drawn from a season five years down the road. The only qualifier is that he must have played in at least 30 games during the season in question, thereby avoiding small-sample-size effects.
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>
         <section class="article_page article_page--secondary js-article-page-3 cf display-none " data-page="3">
          <header class="article_page-header">
           <div class="article_nav-bar br-texture--sudo br-texture--sudo-fade-to-white">

              <span class="aquo">

             </a>
             <div class="paginators_counter">
              3
              <span class="br-light-grey">
               of
              </span>
              <span class="paginators_page-count">
               29
              </span>
             </div>
             <a class="paginator paginator--secondary paginator--next" href="javascript: void(0)" title="Next Page">
              Next
              <span class="aquo">
               
              </span>
             </a>
            </div>
            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             Honorable Mentions
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="Honorable Mentions" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/003/hi-res-c1f869f6570cdfdf964538fd0f3476ca_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Brian Drake/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Mookie Blaylock
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            The Atlanta Hawks' franchise leader in both three-pointers made and steals, Mookie Blaylock was very much a two-way player during the 1990s, despite standing only 6'0". During that stint with the Hawks, as well as his time with the New Jersey Nets and Golden State Warriors, he had a deft shooting touch and quick hands, allowing him to make up for his lack of size on a regular basis.
           </p>
           <p>
            His
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/b/blaylmo01.html#advanced::none">
             6.6 total rebounding percentage
            </a>
            over the course of his career also stands out for a player his size.
           </p>
           <p>
            But while Blaylock has some nice stats in certain areas, he was never much of a scorer and has rather lackluster per-game numbers for the majority of his playing days. He earned just under 14 Career Contributions per season, and it's a bit troubling that he could never advance to the penultimate round of the playoffs at any point in his NBA life.The one-time All-Star is definitely worth a mention, but not much more than that when surrounded by peers of this caliber.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Terrell Brandon
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            Talk about underrated. Earning
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/b/brandte01.html#advanced::none" target="_blank">
             0.147 win shares
            </a>
            per 48 minutes during his playing days, Terrell Brandon actually ranks No. 15 among the 50 point guards who were analyzed in detail for this particular installment of our historical rankings. Problem is, he didn't maintain that rate for too long, even if he was good enough to earn a
            <a href="http://www.si.com/vault/1997/02/10/222602/floor-leader-clevelands-unassuming-terrell-brandon-is-the-nbas-best-pure-point-guard--and-its-best-kept-secret">
             1997
             <em>
              Sports Illustrated
             </em>
             cover
            </a>
            that called him "the best point guard in the NBA."
           </p>
           <p>
            Brandon played in only 724 games over the course of 11 seasons. He entered the league at 21 in 1991 after a two-year career with the Oregon Ducks, but he left the Association after his age-31 season, boasting great stints with the Cleveland Cavaliers, Milwaukee Bucks (for a shorter time) and Minnesota Timberwolves.
           </p>
           <p>
            Injuries took their toll throughout his NBA tenure, cutting his playing days with the Wolves short when his knees just wouldn't heal properly. The Wolves traded him to the Atlanta Hawks in July 2003, but he never suited up for the franchise and was subsequently waived, which prompted his premature retirement.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Fat Lever
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            If any player was going to move from the honorable mentions into the ranked portion of this countdown, it would be Fat Lever.
           </p>
           <p>
            A two-time All-Star with the Denver Nuggets, the 6'3" floor general was by no means overweight, checking in at a meager 170 pounds. But when he wasn't using his creativity, craftiness and incredible basketball intelligence to create offense for himself (largely in the form of mid-range jumpers) or set up his teammates, he would crash the boards.
           </p>
           <p>
            Lever may not have joined Oscar Robertson as the second player to average a triple-double, but he came quite close. In 1988-89, he averaged 19.8 points, 9.3 rebounds and 7.9 assists per game, though the Western Conference was so stacked with talent at the 1 that he wasn't even named an All-Star.
           </p>
           <p>
            Had it not been for a brutal knee injury suffered during his first season with the Dallas Mavericks, Lever likely would've continued racking up numbers, highlights and accolades rather than experiencing a decline in his early 30s.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Andre Miller
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            Talk about a strange career. Andre Miller doesn't have many honors on his resume, as he's never been a part of an All-Star team or made an All-NBA squad. He does have a random 50-point game to his credit, but his legacy will be more about longevity and consistent production for well over a decade.
           </p>
           <p>
            Fresh out of Utah, Miller entered the NBA with the Cleveland Cavaliers right before the turn of the century at the tender young age of 23. Now 39 years old, the point guard is still playing an important role with the Sacramento Kings, continuing to complete lob passes and post up as well as any guard in recent memory.
           </p>
           <p>
            The 2013-14 season was actually the first year in his careerthe lockout-shortened 2011-12 campaign in which he played all 66 games notwithstandingthat he didn't suit up at least 80 times, and that was largely due to philosophical disagreements with first-year Denver Nuggets head coach Brian Shaw.
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Terry Porter
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            Though Terry Porter doesn't stand out in too many areas, he enjoyed a remarkably long career1,274 games played over the course of 17 seasonsand remains one of the more efficient scoring point guards the game has seen.
           </p>
           <p>
            Throughout his NBA tenure, Porter shot 46.3 percent from the field, 38.6 percent from beyond the arc and 83.6 percent from the charity stripe, giving him a lifetime
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/p/portete01.html#advanced::none">
             true shooting percentage of .576
            </a>
            .Among the 50 floor generals studied in detail, that's the No. 8 mark.
           </p>
           <p>
            However, Porter had to pick his spots carefully. His career-best scoring average was only 18.2 points per game, and the backseat role he often occupied didn't allow him to gain all that much league-wide recognition. He was a two-time All-Star during his days with the Portland Trail Blazers, but his resume is completely devoid of any All-NBA honors.
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>
         <section class="article_page article_page--secondary js-article-page-4 cf display-none " data-page="4">
          <header class="article_page-header">
           <div class="article_nav-bar br-texture--sudo br-texture--sudo-fade-to-white">

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             25. Calvin Murphy
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="25. Calvin Murphy" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/004/hi-res-312398799d9f57f9a0d051ce5c5e13ec_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Dick Raphael/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1970-83
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : San Diego/Houston Rockets
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 17.9 points, 2.1 rebounds, 4.4 assists, 1.5 steals, 0.1 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 25.6 points, 3.1 rebounds, 7.4 assists, 1.9 steals, 0.1 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/m/murphca01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 18.0 PER, .536 TS%, 109 ORtg, 107 DRtg, 84.1 WS, 0.132 WS/48, 0 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 602.82 PP, 91.42 AS, 226 CC, 17.38 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.225 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <br/>
            Though his lone All-Star appearance in 1978-79 indicates that Calvin Murphy didn't receive much attention during a career that spanned both the pre- and post-merger portions of NBA history, he was quite valuable all the same. Murphy joined the Association with the San Diego Rockets in 1970, and while the franchise would move to Houston the next year, he never played for a different team.
           </p>
           <p>
            All the while, he earned 17.38 Career Contributions per season, emerging as one of the most valuable players on the Rockets year in and year out. In fact, that mark ranks No. 9 among the 50 studied point guards, as players who suit up at the 1 don't always earn as many win shares as standouts at other positions.
           </p>
           <p>
            As a result of that sustained production for more than 1,000 games during his career, Murphy's total number of Career Contributions (226) ranks No. 14 among the same group of 50. He's one of only nine floor generals with a top-15 finish in both of those categories, though the rest of his resume isn't quite so stellar.
           </p>
           <p>
            The 5'9" Niagara University product wasn't a traditional point guard by any stretch. In fact, he recorded more than five assists per game only twice during his 13-year career. He was much more of a scorer, despite his diminutive frame, but that's by no means being held against him here.
           </p>
           <p>
            The biggest issue is simply a lack of pure dominance, as well as lackluster results from most of the Houston teams he helped lead. In 13 seasons, Murphy advanced to the playoffs only six times, and his 91.42 Advancement Shares are by no means elite.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             24. Jo Jo White
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="24. Jo Jo White" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/005/hi-res-057c53b0b2dddb56e9f1b49d28be60a8_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Dick Raphael/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1969-81
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Boston Celtics, Golden State Warriors, Kansas City Kings
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 17.2 points, 4.0 rebounds, 4.9 assists, 1.3 steals, 0.2 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 23.1 points, 5.6 rebounds, 6.1 assists, 1.6 steals, 0.3 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/w/whitejo01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 14.2 PER, .481 TS%, 95 ORtg, 101 DRtg, 54.0 WS, 0.087 WS/48, 0.007 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1342.4 PP, 628.98 AS, 113.77 CC, 9.48 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.048 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Jo Jo White might not stand out as an individual, but that's largely because he never found as much comfort as he did when wearing that Boston Celtics uniform. Despite playing alongside legends like John Havlicek and Dave Cowens, this point guard averaged 18.8 points per game during his nine full seasons in Beantown, topping out with 23.1 points per game in 1971-72.
           </p>
           <p>
            He didn't experience as much success elsewhere, but by the time he'd racked up seven All-Star appearances and two titles, he didn't exactly need to.
           </p>
           <p>
            Perhaps the most notable aspect of White's game was his ability to take on more responsibility when his team truly needed it. He may have a below-average 14.2 PER during the regular season, but his career postseason PER was slightly higher at 15.1. In fact, during his 80 playoff games, the floor general averaged 21.5 points, 4.4 rebounds and 5.7 assists per contest.
           </p>
           <p>
            Plus, he won in the playoffs. He won a lot in fact.
           </p>
           <p>
            White's 628.98 Advancement Shares put him in elite company, as only nine analyzed point guards have earned more over the course of their respective careers. And while his No. 19 finish in Playoff Performance indicates that he received quite a bit of help along the way, that's still a rather impressive figure.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             23. Dave Bing
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="23. Dave Bing" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/009/hi-res-54e3b68497e7a9f7203fd17ef14f7039_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Dick Raphael/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1966-78
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Detroit Pistons, Washington Bullets, Boston Celtics
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 20.3 points, 3.8 rebounds, 6.0 assists, 1.3 steals, 0.2 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 27.1 points, 5.0 rebounds, 7.8 assists, 1.5 steals, 0.3 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/b/bingda01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 17.6 PER, .502 TS%, 99 ORtg, 100 DRtg, 68.8 WS, 0.101 WS/48, 0.386 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 352.16 PP, 0 AS, 172.45 CC, 14.37 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.083 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Sometimes thought of as a shooting guard,
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/b/bingda01.html">
             Basketball-Reference.com
            </a>
            lists Dave Bing as a floor general in 11 of his 12 seasons, so that's where he qualifies for these rankings. And it makes sense, given how good
            <span>
             this 6'3" backcourt player
            </span>
            was at distributing the ball throughout his career.
           </p>
           <p>
            From a per-game standpoint, Bing's best passing season came in 1972-73, when he averaged 7.8 assists per contest for the Detroit Pistons. You could also argue that he was even better two years later, when he produced "only" 7.7 dimes during his typical outing but recorded a career-best 28.9 assist percentage.
           </p>
           <p>
            Over the course of his career, Bing's assist percentage finished at 24.3 percent. It's by no means a standout mark, actually ranking only No. 43 among the 50 point guards broken down in detail while researching, but it's a strong mark for a player who was primarily a scorer.
           </p>
           <p>
            After averaging 20 points per game during his rookie season with the Pistons, this Hall of Famer wouldn't see his scoring average dip into the teens until the 1973-74 campaign, which came seven years later. He peaked as a sophomore, but his numbers remained strong throughout his career, with the exception of his quick decline once he left the Motor City behind and joined the Washington Bullets.
           </p>
           <p>
            Though Bing never found any playoff success and struggled to earn win shares because he so often found himself on limited teams, it was still difficult to find many better guards throughout the late 1960s and '70s.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             22. Rajon Rondo
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="22. Rajon Rondo" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/007/hi-res-540be38a255d652296aaf8d8bdf5dedd_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Brian Babineau/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 2006-Current
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Boston Celtics, Dallas Mavericks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 10.9 points, 4.7 rebounds, 8.4 assists, 1.8 steals, 0.1 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 13.7 points, 5.7 rebounds, 11.7 assists, 2.3 steals, 0.2 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/r/rondora01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 16.8 PER, .503 TS%, 104 ORtg, 103 DRtg, 46.1 WS, 0.12 WS/48, 0.014 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1362.52 PP, 281.41 AS, 100.63 CC, 12.58 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.048 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Rajon Rondo is still attempting to prove that he can lead a team deep into the playoffs without being surrounded by superstars, though he's still not getting that chance while paired up with other big-name players on the Dallas Mavericks. But even as he struggles with that endeavor, he remains one of the best floor generals in the era often hyped up as the golden age of point guards.
           </p>
           <p>
            No. 9's defense is simply sublime, and he's one of the most creative and effective distributors the sport has ever witnessed. Sure, he struggles with his shot and only occasionally pulls off that dazzling behind-the-back fake that twists up defenders, but he holds his own on the offensive end because he can make even lackluster teammates look like quality scorers.
           </p>
           <p>
            Excluding the first two seasons of Rondo's career, when he was transitioning from Kentucky to the rigors of the professional game, his
            <em>
             worst
            </em>
            assist percentage is 39.7. That's higher than the career average of all but five of the studied point guards, and his current assist percentage throughout his Beantown tenure puts him at No. 4 among the same group.
           </p>
           <p>
            Rondo, still playing out his age-28 season (he just turned 29 on Feb. 22), has plenty of time to add to his resume, and steering the Mavericks deep into the playoffs would do wonders for his cause as an individual. But entering the 2014-15 campaign, he had already crossed the 500-game threshold, won a title alongside Ray Allen,
            <a href="http://bleacherreport.com/paul-pierce">
             Paul Pierce
            </a>
            and
            <a href="http://bleacherreport.com/kevin-garnett">
             Kevin Garnett
            </a>
            and asserted himself as one of the premier players at his position during an era dominated by 1-guards.
           </p>
           <p>
            That's quite the set of accomplishments.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             21. Sam Cassell
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="21. Sam Cassell" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/006/hi-res-93d40bae2cfc8c1d4161f99f825abd1d_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Andrew D. Bernstein/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1993-2008
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Houston Rockets, Phoenix Suns, Dallas Mavericks, New Jersey Nets, Milwaukee Bucks, Minnesota Timberwolves, Los Angeles Clippers, Boston Celtics
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 15.7 points, 3.2 rebounds, 6.0 assists, 1.1 steals, 0.2 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 19.8 points, 4.4 rebounds, 9.0 assists, 1.6 steals, 0.3 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/c/cassesa01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 19.5 PER, .544 TS%, 110 ORtg, 108 DRtg, 87.5 WS, 0.141 WS/48, 0.003 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1181.84 PP, 343.48 AS, 205.72 CC, 13.71 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.059 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            During the 993 games he played in the Association, Sam Cassell became a rather well-traveled point guard. He was traded seven times throughout his career, even playing for the Phoenix Suns, Dallas Mavericks and New Jersey Nets all during the 1996-97 season.
           </p>
           <p>
            Historically transcendent standouts usually aren't traded in their prime, but Cassell's placement in these rankings isn't about his peak season. After all, he never averaged 20 points per game, nor did he break into double figures in the assist column over the course of a campaign.
           </p>
           <p>
            His legacy is more about longevity and playing at a high level throughout his career. A 19.5 PER is nothing to laugh at, especially when the player who produced it hovered right around that level for over a decade. In fact, Cassell's PER was on the right side of 20 for the entirety of a seven-season stretch, and it's not as though he was filling minor roles for his teams.
           </p>
           <p>
            He only have earned a single All-Star selection, but isn't it telling that his lone honor came during his age-34 season? His titles came during his role-playing years, both early in his career with the Houston Rockets and late in his NBA life with the Boston Celtics, but in between, he was a consistent standout who played efficient and effective basketball.
           </p>
           <p>
            Cassell isn't usually one of the first names you think of when pondering the great point guards in the history of this league, but he's earned such a status after putting together so many excellentthough not quite fantasticcampaigns.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             20. Mark Price
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="20. Mark Price" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/008/hi-res-8d602273c9fb1afd74c0e01a96cf4ddc_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Mike Powell/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1986-98
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Cleveland Cavaliers, Washington Bullets, Golden State Warriors, Orlando Magic
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 15.2 points, 2.6 rebounds, 6.7 assists, 1.2 steals, 0.1 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 19.6 points, 3.4 rebounds, 10.4 assists, 1.6 steals, 0.2 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/p/pricema01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 19.6 PER, .586 TS%, 116 ORtg, 109 DRtg, 71.1 WS, 0.158 WS/48, 0.107 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 672.57 PP, 36.98 AS, 162.22 CC, 13.52 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.273 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Mark Price could flat-out shoot the basketball.
           </p>
           <p>
            While he was a top-notch distributor during his prime with the Cleveland Cavaliers and could provide his squad with a decent amount of thefts during the average game, there was no doubt his primary skill was stroking jumpers from all areas of the court. Price never averaged 20 points per game; instead, he just made the most of the opportunities he was given.
           </p>
           <p>
            In addition to shooting 47.2 percent from the field throughout his career, Price remains one of the select players in NBA history who made both 40 percent of his triples and 90 percent of his freebies. In fact, only he and Steve Nash qualify for that
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/play-index/psl_finder.cgi?request=1&amp;match=combined&amp;type=totals&amp;per_minute_base=36&amp;per_poss_base=100&amp;lg_id=NBA&amp;is_playoffs=N&amp;year_min=&amp;year_max=&amp;franch_id=&amp;season_start=1&amp;season_end=-1&amp;age_min=0&amp;age_max=99&amp;height_min=0&amp;height_max=99&amp;shoot_hand=&amp;birth_country_is=Y&amp;birth_country=&amp;is_active=&amp;is_hof=&amp;is_as=&amp;as_comp=gt&amp;as_val=&amp;pos_is_g=Y&amp;pos_is_gf=Y&amp;pos_is_f=Y&amp;pos_is_fg=Y&amp;pos_is_fc=Y&amp;pos_is_c=Y&amp;pos_is_cf=Y&amp;qual=&amp;c1stat=fg3_pct&amp;c1comp=gt&amp;c1val=.4&amp;c2stat=ft_pct&amp;c2comp=gt&amp;c2val=.9&amp;c3stat=g&amp;c3comp=gt&amp;c3val=100&amp;c4stat=&amp;c4comp=gt&amp;c4val=&amp;c5stat=&amp;c5comp=gt&amp;c6mult=1.0&amp;c6stat=&amp;order_by=ws">
             exclusive club
            </a>
            , at least among players with more than 100 games to their credit.Thanks to his 1988-89 season (one of his four All-Star campaigns), Price is also a member of the 50-40-90 club, gaining entry while averaging 18.9 points per game.
           </p>
           <p>
            With a career true shooting percentage of .586, Price ranks as the No. 5 point guard among the 50 analyzed, trailing only Magic Johnson, John Stockton, Nash and
            <a href="http://bleacherreport.com/stephen-curry">
             Stephen Curry
            </a>
            .
           </p>
           <p>
            Unfortunately, the performance metrics work against him. Price may have led his team in win shares during the 1987-88, 1988-89, 1989-90 and 1993-94 seasons, but he peaked at just 11.3 during the second of those four go-rounds. His 13.52 Career Contributions per season are decidedly lackluster, and while he steered Cleveland into the playoffs many times, he could never find much success after the 82nd game of the season.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             19. Tim Hardaway
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="19. Tim Hardaway" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/011/hi-res-21399f0c8f2a953b4497f5f0b4cb303b_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              NBA Photos/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1989-2003
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Golden State Warriors, Miami Heat, Dallas Mavericks, Denver Nuggets, Indiana Pacers
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 17.7 points, 3.3 rebounds, 8.2 assists, 1.6 steals, 0.1 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 23.4 points, 4.0 rebounds, 10.6 assists, 2.6 steals, 0.2 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/h/hardati01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 18.6 PER, .530 TS%, 110 ORtg, 108 DRtg, 85.0 WS, 0.133 WS/48, 0.336 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 703.36 PP, 42.92 AS, 200.29 CC, 15.41 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.264 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            It's all about that devastating crossover, one so deadly it earned its own nickname. Countless defenders fell prey to the "UTEP two-step," and Tim Hardaway was good enough once he worked his way into space that he could torture the opposition with both his scoring and distributing abilities.
           </p>
           <p>
            That may not seem special now, but back in the late 1980s and early '90s, Hardaway was an innovator, as this passage from
            <a href="http://www.nba.com/2011/news/features/shaun_powell/07/12/hardaway-cross/">
             NBA.com's Shaun Powell
            </a>
            helped explain in 2011:
           </p>
           <blockquote>
            <p>
             No question, Hardaway is one of a handful of pioneers who changed the game forever, and it tickles him, even now, to be remembered in those terms.
            </p>
            <p>
             "That's great," he said. "The people who run the NBA draft combine even have a crossover drill for the prospects. It makes me laugh. I was just a college player looking to try something new, and came up with this. And now they're having all these college players prove they can do it."
            </p>
            <p>
             That dribble has frozen more human beings than a Slurpee. He used the dribble to last 15 ankle-breaking years in the NBA, with great success in Golden State and Miami, and left a legacy that you can see on any court, on any level. Go left. Then right. Or vice versa. There are several variations now; the "killer" crossover between the legs (Hardaway's specialty), a "normal" crossover, a "half" crossover (fake) and so on. It sounds so simple, but it took many hours in the laboratory for Hardaway to develop.
            </p>
           </blockquote>
           <p>
            Hardaway's crossover was his signature move, but it was only one specialty in a deep bag of tricks. After all, you can't earn over 200 Career Contributions without doing anything more than breaking ankles, nor can you put together 0.336 MVP Shares.
           </p>
           <p>
            That alone says a lot, as Hardaway was such a respected player on some high-quality teamsmost famously the "Run TMC" Golden State Warriorsthat he earned MVP votes in four different years. During three of those seasons (1991-92, 1996-97 and 1997-78), he was a top-10 finisher, highlighted by earning the fourth-most votes in 1997.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             18. Lenny Wilkens
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="18. Lenny Wilkens" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/012/hi-res-f58ede4e196226d70b56c2175820d7d0_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Anonymous/Associated Press
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1960-75
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : St. Louis Hawks, Seattle SuperSonics, Cleveland Cavaliers, Portland Trail Blazers
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 16.5 points, 4.7 rebounds, 6.7 assists, 1.3 steals, 0.2 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 22.4 points, 6.2 rebounds, 9.6 assists, 1.3 steals, 0.2 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/w/wilkele01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 16.8 PER, .511 TS%, 100 DRtg, 95.5 WS, 0.120 WS/48, 0.323 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 851.2 PP, 231.62 AS, 240.92 CC, 16.06 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.559 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Lenny Wilkens may not have any All-NBA selections to match up against Tim Hardaway's five, nor was he quite so dominant as an individual. However, he comes in just ahead of the crossover machine because he played for a significantly longer time (210 additional games in fact) and was more valuable.
           </p>
           <p>
            Throughout his impressive NBA career, most notably his All-Star days with the St. Louis Hawks and Seattle SuperSonics, Wilkens earned 16.06 Career Contributions per season. And that's a number dragged down by only playing 20 games during his sophomore year.
           </p>
           <p>
            The point guard's most valuable season actually came in 1972-73, when he earned 9.5 win shares for the 32-win Cleveland Cavaliers. The result was a total of 28.18 Career Contributions, which placed him at No. 7 in the league-wide hierarchy. That played a big part in his 0.559 LMVP shares, which remain the No. 11 mark among the studied point guards.
           </p>
           <p>
            Wilkens did a lot of work in the playoffs to get ahead of Hardaway. The 6'1" southpaw advanced into the postseason seven times, all of which came during his time with the Hawks. Though he'd never win a ring, he at least came close on multiple occasions, primarily when he made it to the 1961 NBA Finals.
           </p>
           <p>
            The Hawks were a constant fixture in the playoffs prior to the ABA-NBA merger. In fact, from 1956 through 1973, they only failed to make it past the regular season once.
           </p>
           <p>
            It's no coincidence that year (1962) was the same one that Wilkens was limited to just 20 games.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>
