            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             17. Maurice Cheeks
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="17. Maurice Cheeks" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/010/hi-res-906fa717970b62841b950276cb42cf66_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Andrew D. Bernstein/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1978-93
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Philadelphia 76ers, San Antonio Spurs, New York Knicks, Atlanta Hawks, New Jersey Nets
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 11.1 points, 2.8 rebounds, 6.7 assists, 2.1 steals, 0.3 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 15.6 points, 3.5 rebounds, 9.2 assists, 2.6 steals, 0.5 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/c/cheekma01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 16.5 PER, .572 TS%, 116 ORtg, 105 DRtg, 103.5 WS, 0.143 WS/48, 0 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1871.31 PP, 424.95 AS, 214.26 CC, 14.28 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            As an accumulator of stats, Maurice Cheeks does not deserve to be called one of the 20 greatest point guards of all time. After all, his career PER is only slightly above the league average of 15, he never led any of his teams in win shares (hence the zero LMVP shares) and his scoring figures were never all that impressive.
           </p>
           <p>
            But Cheeks' game was never about traditional measures. Instead, he thrived on the defensive end of the floor.
           </p>
           <p>
            Throughout his career, he made the All-Defensive squad on five separate occasions, all of which came during the mid-'80s while he was playing with the Philadelphia 76ers. As
            <a href="http://www.csnphilly.com/basketball-philadelphia-sixers/why-isnt-maurice-cheeks-hall-fame" target="_blank">
             CSNPhilly.com's John Finger
            </a>
            wrotein April 2014 while looking at Cheeks' Hall of Fame credentials, "
            <span>
             When he retired, Cheeks was the NBA's all-time leader in steals and was second behind Oscar Robertson in all-time assists. He still ranks fifth in steals and 10th in assists these days, and was a four-time All-Star and four-time first team All-Defense."
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            With some of the best and quickest hands in NBA history, Cheeks became a key contributor to plenty of strong squads. Most notable among them was the 1982-83 Philadelphia team that helped him win the first and only title of his career. True to form, the point guard was up to the challenge during that postseason, averaging 16.3 points, 3.0 rebounds, 7.0 assists and 2.0 steals per game in his 13 appearances.
           </p>
           <p>
            No featured point guard in these rankings has averaged fewer points per game, but Cheeks' game was never about the final number in the scoring column.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             16. Dennis Johnson
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="16. Dennis Johnson" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/013/hi-res-fc57e36c768c16f9099bfc54970e9ca0_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Brian Drake/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1976-90
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Seattle SuperSonics, Phoenix Suns, Boston Celtics
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 14.1 points, 3.9 rebounds, 5.0 assists, 1.3 steals, 0.6 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 19.5 points, 5.1 rebounds, 7.8 assists, 1.8 steals, 1.2 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/j/johnsde01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 14.6 PER, .511 TS%, 107 ORtg, 105 DRtg, 82.6 WS, 0.110 WS/48, 0.084 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 2349 PP, 932.61 AS, 167.81 CC, 11.99 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.043 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Speaking of good defenders, Dennis Johnson was even better than Maurice Cheeks on that end of the floor.
           </p>
           <p>
            Though his offensive game lagged behind Cheeks'thanks to his shooting efficiency (that .511 true shooting percentage does
            <em>
             not
            </em>
            stand out in a good way)and he had lackluster passing numbers despite being more heavily involved in the offense, he was, without question, one of the very best defenders to ever line up at the 1. His nine All-Defensive selections were a testament to that, as they leave him tied with two other players for the all-time record among point guards.
           </p>
           <p>
            Frankly though, the difference between the two floor generals' defensive skills isn't as big as the gap between their offensive games. So that's not all that pushes Johnson slightly ahead and puts him on the verge of breaking into the top 15.
           </p>
           <p>
            It's actually his playoff resume that does the trick, as Johnson played a crucial part on so many great postseason squads. He won a ring while playing over 40 minutes per game in 1979 with the Seattle SuperSonics, and he added two more while teaming up with Larry Bird, Kevin McHale and Robert Parish on the Boston Celtics.
           </p>
           <p>
            At the end of his career, he'd racked up a 2,349 Playoff Performance score and 932.61 Advancement Shares. Among the 50 analyzed point guards, that leaves him ranked No. 5 and No. 7 respectively. Compare that to Cheeks, who came in at No. 11 and No. 14 in the two categories, and you can see why it's Johnson moving ahead by a slim margin.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             15. Tiny Archibald
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="15. Tiny Archibald" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/014/hi-res-53387d1ab2f2b255277fa86435c370f4_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              NBA Photos/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1970-84
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Cincinnati Royals, Kansas City Kings, New York Nets, Boston Celtics, Milwaukee Bucks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 18.8 points, 2.3 rebounds, 7.4 assists, 1.1 steals, 0.1 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 34.0 points, 3.0 rebounds, 11.4 assists, 1.7 steals, 0.3 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/a/architi01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 18.0 PER, .543 TS%, 109 ORtg, 105 DRtg, 83.4 WS, 0.128 WS/48, 0.465 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 452.61 PP, 269.07 AS, 211.87 CC, 16.3 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 1.31 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Only one player in NBA history has led the league in both scoring and distributing during a single season: Nate "Tiny" Archibald.
           </p>
           <p>
            During his 1972-73 campaign with the Kansas City-Omaha Kings, he averaged an NBA-best 34.0 points and 11.4 assists per game, even shooting 48.8 percent from the field. It was quite obviously the top season of his career, and his 25.2 PER that year was just one of the four times in which he finished over 20.
           </p>
           <p>
            Archibald's true prime lasted only six years, but he was pretty darn good during that stretch.
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/a/architi01.html#1972-1977-sum:per_game">
             From 1971 through 1977
            </a>
            , he averaged 26.7 points and 8.6 assists per game, which put him on pace to finish far better than No. 15 in these historical rankings. Sadly, he tore his Achilles during his first season with the New York Nets, limiting him to just 34 games played in 1976-77.
           </p>
           <p>
            The multitalented point guard would miss the entire 1977-78 campaign, and he was never the same after he took his talents to Beantown and joined the Boston Celtics. Still a distributing threat, he lacked the explosive scoring that had previously made him so special, which puts a damper on the overall nature of his career.
           </p>
           <p>
            Still, at his best, Archibald was without question one of the 10 best floor generals the Association has witnessed. During that famous season in which he led the league in two prominent categories, he earned 14.2 win shares on a 36-win Kings team, giving him 37.53 Career Contributions in just one season. One year prior, he'd actually earned 40.04 Career Contributions, largely because his 12.9 win shares looked even better on a squad that went just 30-52.
           </p>
           <p>
            Even with the steep decline after his devastating injury cutting his production short, Archibald still managed to finish with 1.31 LMVP Shares due primarily to his early years. Only 40 players in NBA history have earned more, and just four of them played the same position as this 6'1" offensive monster.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             14. Chauncey Billups
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="14. Chauncey Billups" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/016/hi-res-9a468bf344616604958f87dd29083d6e_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Joe Murphy/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1997-2014
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Boston Celtics, Toronto Raptors,
            <span>
             Denver Nuggets, Minnesota Timberwolves, Detroit Pistons, New York Knicks, Los Angeles Clippers
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 15.2 points, 2.9 rebounds, 5.4 assists, 1.0 steals, 0.2 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 19.5 points, 3.7 rebounds, 8.6 assists, 1.3 steals, 0.3 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/b/billuch01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 18.8 PER, .580 TS%, 118 ORtg, 107 DRtg, 120.8 WS, 0.176 WS/48, 0.375 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 2044 PP, 477.4 AS, 259.15 CC, 15.24 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.461 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Chauncey Billups has to be one of the most misunderstood players in NBA history.
           </p>
           <p>
            He's not traditionally thought of as a top-15 point guard of all time, and that's wrong. He's also given the "Mr. Big Shot" nickname because he drained a handful of key shots, even though his performance in the clutch would indicate that a more appropriate moniker is "Mr. Big Miss."
           </p>
           <p>
            "W
            <span>
             e remember great plays often at the expense of not so great plays,"
             <a href="http://pistonpowered.com/2010/09/21/myth-chauncey-billups-is-really-mr-big-shot/">
              Piston Powered's Patrick Hayes
             </a>
             wrote while debunking the clutch myth for this particular point guard. "Billups was a well-below-league-average shooter in game-winning situations, yet his '
            </span>
            Mr. Big Shot' nickname stuck because he hit four or five really memorable shots as a Piston."
           </p>
           <p>
            But an inaccurate nickname only matters so much. It's just one of the many ways Billups' career isn't analyzed properly.
           </p>
           <p>
            His most famous contributions came during the 2003-04 season, when he steered the Detroit Pistons to a title and won Finals MVP. It's a team commonly cited as one that didn't roster a true star, but that's disrespectful to Billups, who earned 11.3 win shares that season and would get into double digits during each of his following five campaigns.
           </p>
           <p>
            Maybe he wasn't a "star" by the conclusion of the 2004 title run, but he certainly became one and played like such throughout the season in question.
           </p>
           <p>
            After a long career, the point guardone who became Denver's "King of Park Hill" and still receives pure adoration from fans when he shows up in the Pepsi Centerretired having played in over 1,000 games, and he still boasted a lifetime PER of 18.8. Perhaps even more impressively, he earned 120.8 win shares and collected 0.176 per 48 minutes, which ranks him No. 8 and No. 7, respectively, among the 50 studied floor generals.
           </p>
           <p>
            Billups' big-shooting ways may have been more myth than fact, but the rest of his career was better than advertised, which more than makes up the difference.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             13. Kevin Johnson
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="13. Kevin Johnson" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/015/hi-res-0e1885dd7df3dd626c88acb7ab6b61d8_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Andrew D. Bernstein/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1987-2000
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Cleveland Cavaliers, Phoenix Suns
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 17.9 points, 3.3 rebounds, 9.1 assists, 1.5 steals, 0.2 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 22.5 points, 4.2 rebounds, 12.2 assists, 2.1 steals, 0.4 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/j/johnske02.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 20.7 PER, .585 TS%, 118 ORtg, 109 DRtg, 92.8 WS, 0.178 WS/48, 0.063 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1747.2 PP, 164 AS, 201.3 CC, 16.77 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.425 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Kevin Johnson was basically offense personified during his prime days with the Phoenix Suns. After the Cleveland Cavaliers
            <span>
             traded him to the desert halfway through his rookie season, he didn't take long asserting himself as a premier scoring and passing threat.
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            As a sophomore, Johnson averaged 20.4 points and 12.2 assists per game. He remains
            <a href="http://bkref.com/tiny/eIeG5">
             one of only two players
            </a>
            in NBA history to join the 20-10 club in one of his first two seasons, with Oscar Robertson serving as the other member. In fact,
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/play-index/psl_finder.cgi?request=1&amp;match=single&amp;type=totals&amp;per_minute_base=36&amp;per_poss_base=100&amp;lg_id=NBA&amp;is_playoffs=N&amp;year_min=&amp;year_max=2014&amp;franch_id=&amp;season_start=1&amp;season_end=5&amp;age_min=0&amp;age_max=99&amp;height_min=0&amp;height_max=99&amp;shoot_hand=&amp;birth_country_is=Y&amp;birth_country=&amp;is_active=&amp;is_hof=&amp;is_as=&amp;as_comp=gt&amp;as_val=&amp;pos_is_g=Y&amp;pos_is_gf=Y&amp;pos_is_f=Y&amp;pos_is_fg=Y&amp;pos_is_fc=Y&amp;pos_is_c=Y&amp;pos_is_cf=Y&amp;qual=&amp;c1stat=pts_per_g&amp;c1comp=gt&amp;c1val=20&amp;c2stat=ast_per_g&amp;c2comp=gt&amp;c2val=10&amp;c3stat=&amp;c3comp=gt&amp;c3val=&amp;c4stat=&amp;c4comp=gt&amp;c4val=&amp;c5stat=&amp;c5comp=gt&amp;c6mult=1.0&amp;c6stat=&amp;order_by=ws">
             just six players
            </a>
            have ever done so in one of their first five seasonsJohnson, Robertson, Tiny Archibald, Tim Hardaway, Chris Paul and Isiah Thomasand Johnson, Robertson and Thomas are the only ones with three such qualified years.
           </p>
           <p>
            For a nine-year stretch
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/j/johnske02.html#1989-1997-sum:per_game">
             from 1988 through 1997
            </a>
            , the dynamic point guard actually averaged 19.8 points and 10.0 dimes per contest, leaving no doubt just how good he was on that end of the floor.
           </p>
           <p>
            Even factoring in his quick decline during his 30s, Johnson retired with a 20.7 PER (No. 7 among the 50 studied point guards). His true shooting percentage was undeniably elite, and he balanced that with a lifetime assist percentage of 38.8 percent, one topped by only seven members of the same group. Factor in earning more win shares per 48 minutes than all but five floor generals, and you can see why he ranks this high despite playing only 735 games.
           </p>
           <p>
            Multiple hidden herniaswhich led to other injury problemsprevented him from thriving alongside Charles Barkley once the big man joined him on the Phoenix roster, and they ultimately pushed the 1-guard to retire prematurely. He attempted a comeback at 33 years old, but it lasted only six games, as he clearly wasn't the same player.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             12. Tony Parker
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="12. Tony Parker" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/017/hi-res-7c9af2277f28ce30c05f011356458f6c_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Barry Gossage/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 2001-Current
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : San Antonio Spurs
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 17.0 points, 2.9 rebounds, 5.9 assists, 0.9 steals, 0.1 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 22.0 points, 3.7 rebounds, 7.7 assists, 1.2 steals, 0.1 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/p/parketo01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 18.9 PER, .550 TS%, 109 ORtg, 105 DRtg, 99.2 WS, 0.148 WS/48, 0.379 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 2481.36 PP, 837.42 AS, 191.15 CC, 14.7 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.079 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Tony Parker has always played in Gregg Popovich's vaunted system with the San Antonio Spurs, and that's both a good and bad thing. The good is that it's helped him and his team thrive during both the regular season and the playoffs, while the bad is that it's prevented him from standing out more as an individual. Had Parker played for another team, he could've been more selfish and earned better statistics, but he probably wouldn't have won four rings.
           </p>
           <p>
            The French floor general's playoff numbers are undeniably elite. His 2,481.36 Playoff Performance score ranks No. 4 among all 1-guards, and his 837.42 Advancement Shares put him at No. 8 among the same group.
           </p>
           <p>
            It's tough to argue with that resume, especially since Parker has still put up great regular-season numbers all the while. Though he still hasn't developed a truly potent perimeter shot, he's picked his spots wisely throughout his career and managed to shoot quite efficiently. He's also a deft passer with a knack for getting into the lane, and that's helped him earn 0.147 win shares per 48 minutes during his ongoing NBA life.
           </p>
           <p>
            Amazingly enough, he even led the Spurs in win shares twice (7.1 in 2011-12 and 9.3 in 2012-13). It's amazing not because he lacks the talent to accomplish such a feat, but because he's played with Tim Duncan throughout the entirety of his increasingly storied career.
           </p>
           <p>
            Could Parker have experienced more individual glory elsewhere? Perhaps.
           </p>
           <p>
            Nonetheless, here's guessing he has no regrets.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             11. Chris Paul
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="11. Chris Paul" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/018/hi-res-c9f64c8cd9d5a74be406e95a9199df01_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Ezra Shaw/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 2005-Current
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : New Orleans Hornets, Los Angeles Clippers
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 18.6 points, 4.4 rebounds, 9.9 assists, 2.3 steals, 0.1 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 22.8 points, 5.5 rebounds, 11.6 assists, 2.8 steals, 0.2 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/p/paulch01.html#advanced::none" target="_blank">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 25.5 PER, .576 TS%, 123 ORtg, 105 DRtg, 127.8 WS, 0.246 WS/48, 1.459 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1047.81 PP, 0 AS, 257.46 CC, 28.61 CC/Season, 2 LMVPs, 3.05 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            With just 617 games under his belt heading into the 2014-15 season, there's only so high Chris Paul can climb at this stage of his career. However, he's on pace to finish well within the top five by the time he retires, assuming his NBA tenure follows a typical trajectory.
           </p>
           <p>
            Some playoff success would help as well, since Paul is one of just four players either featured or ranked as an honorable mention in this countdown who has never advanced as far as the conference finals.That's not exactly his fault, though, as his Playoff Performance score of 1,047.81 ranks in the top half of the studied floor generals, and his 25.0 postseason PER is undeniably elite. In fact, Paul has actually led the playoffs in PER three times.
           </p>
           <p>
            But even without a ring to his credit (or a deep playoff run for that matter), the Los Angeles Clippers 1-guard has been ridiculously good.
           </p>
           <p>
            Only Oscar Robertson has earned more Career Contributions per season, and despite still being in his 20s, Paul is already in the top 10 for total Career Contributions at his position. He's one of only three point guards to win LMVP, and he's the only one to do so twice. He already ranks No. 14 all time in LMVP shares, and, once more, only Robertson is ahead of him in that category.
           </p>
           <p>
            There's more.
           </p>
           <p>
            Paul ranks third among the 50 point guards in both steals and assists per game. His PER leads the field, and John Stockton is the only 1-guard with a higher assist percentage. No one has a better offensive rating or earned more win shares per 48 minutes. Only eight players (all of whom have spent far more time in the Association) have collected more total win shares. Paul even ranks No. 5 in MVP shares despite still playing out his prime.
           </p>
           <p>
            His resume is already sensational, and it's only going to keep getting better.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             10. Steve Nash
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="10. Steve Nash" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/019/hi-res-e9e6dff8e53d7971608835fe2daf68c8_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Nathaniel S. Butler/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1996-2014
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Phoenix Suns, Dallas Mavericks, Los Angeles Lakers
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 14.3 points, 3.0 rebounds, 8.5 assists, 0.7 steals, 0.1 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 18.8 points, 4.2 rebounds, 11.6 assists, 1.0 steals, 0.2 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/n/nashst01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 20.0 PER, .605 TS%, 118 ORtg, 111 DRtg, 129.7 WS, 0.164 WS/48, 2.429 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1795.2 PP, 157.04 AS, 275.52 CC, 15.31 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.117 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            It might seem blasphemous to have Steve Nash "only" check in at No. 10 in the historical rankings of point guards, but there are no bonus points awarded for being a good guy, entertaining crowds and generally becoming one of the most fun players to watch in NBA history. Style is a nice addition to a resume, but substance matters most.
           </p>
           <p>
            Obviously, there's a lot of substance on Nash's resume, especially with those two MVP awards he earned during his tenure with the Phoenix Sunsexcept we tend to give him a bit too much credit for engineering those offenses, as he was surrounded by the perfect complementary pieces.
           </p>
           <p>
            Shawn Marion, for example, is remarkably undervalued.
           </p>
           <p>
            Nash, even with those pair of MVPs, earned just 15.31 Career Contributions per season, which checks in behind 18 other floor generals who were among the 50 studied for these rankings. The length of his careereven counting his unfortunate decline with the Los Angeles Lakersallowed him to earn 275.22 total Career Contributions, and that's the No. 6 mark among the same group.
           </p>
           <p>
            Couple that with only 0.117 LMVP Shares and a lackluster playoff resume that features precious few deep runs through the Western Conference, and it becomes a bit more clear why, despite the subjective desires of those who watched him excel during his prime years, it's hard to rank him much higher when sticking to the objective pieces of the puzzle.
           </p>
           <p>
            Nash is a distributing and shooting legend, and he's one of the most creative offensive geniuses who has ever stepped foot on an NBA floor. There's no doubt he's an all-time great, but it's easy to get carried away and try to make him something more than he actually was, especially without defense giving him any advantages on the field.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>
         <section class="article_page article_page--secondary js-article-page-20 cf display-none " data-page="20">
          <header class="article_page-header">
           <div class="article_nav-bar br-texture--sudo br-texture--sudo-fade-to-white">

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             9. Jason Kidd
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="9. Jason Kidd" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/020/hi-res-ac74ea4e8612b9a55b2b9b583c3f1a1c_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Ron Hoskins/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1994-2013
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Dallas Mavericks, Phoenix Suns, New Jersey Nets, New York Knicks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 12.6 points, 6.3 rebounds, 8.7 assists, 1.9 steals, 0.3 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 18.7 points, 8.2 rebounds, 10.8 assists, 2.3 steals, 0.5 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/k/kiddja01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 17.9 PER, .507 TS%, 107 ORtg, 102 DRtg, 138.6 WS, 0.133 WS/48, 0.933 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 2156.7 PP, 348.24 AS, 320.66 CC, 18.88 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.625 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            For a non-scorer like Jason Kidd with a remarkably lengthy career to average 18.88 Career Contributions per season, the No. 12 mark among all analyzed 1-guards, something special must have been happening.
           </p>
           <p>
            And for Kidd, it was.
           </p>
           <p>
            Even overlooking his passing, as impressive as it may have been, he still stands out in three areasrebounding, defense and improvement.
           </p>
           <p>
            As for the first, he averaged a remarkable 6.3 boards per game throughout his career despite standing only 6'4". Highlighted by a career-best per-game mark of 8.2, earned when he was already well into his 30s, he compiled a career total rebounding percentage of 10.0.
           </p>
           <p>
            That's an absolutely incredible rate for a point guard, and it leaves him trailing only Magic Johnson and Fat Lever among the same group of studied point guards that has been referenced so many times.
           </p>
           <p>
            Kidd's defense was nothing to sniff at either.
           </p>
           <p>
            He was selected to the All-Defensive squad nine times throughout his 19-year career, consistently swiping the ball away from the opposition and making his team much better on the less glamorous end. Not once in his career, whether at the beginning when he was transitioning from California or at the end when he was nearing 40 years old, did his defensive box plus-minus slip below zero, which indicates he was without fail a plus on that side of the ball.
           </p>
           <p>
            And as for improvement, it's part of what made him so special, allowing him to enjoy a much longer career than he would have otherwise.
            <span>
             The easiest way to see it was with his perimeter shooting. He was awful when he first entered the Association and would go on to become one of the most efficient snipers in the league, even if his overall scoring numbers and percentages remained mediocre at best.
            </span>
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             8. Gary Payton
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="8. Gary Payton" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/021/hi-res-d5786725322823b2e29142081e4e097d_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Barry Gossage/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1990-2007
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Seattle SuperSonics, Milwaukee Bucks, Los Angeles Lakers, Boston Celtics, Miami Heat
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 16.3 points, 3.9 rebounds, 6.7 assists, 1.8 steals, 0.2 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 24.2 points, 6.5 rebounds, 9.0 assists, 2.9 steals, 0.3 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/p/paytoga01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 18.9 PER, .528 TS%, 111 ORtg, 106 DRtg, 145.5 WS, 0.148 WS/48, 0.823 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1721.72 PP, 303.07 AS, 321.66 CC, 18.92 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.902 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Gary Payton joins Jason Kidd as one of only two point guards in NBA history to gain entry to the All-Defensive squads at least nine times in a career. Except unlike the man who comes in just behind him, Payton also won Defensive Player of the Year.
           </p>
           <p>
            He was also more valuable to his teams.
           </p>
           <p>
            Payton, though without question one of the great stoppers in the history of this sport, was by no means a one-way player. He might not have rebounded as well as Kidd or dished out so many dimes, but he was a dangerous scorer and a versatile contributor, one who earned 18.92 Career Contributions per season. Among the 50 studied floor generals, only Chris Paul and the top five players in these rankings fared better in that category.
           </p>
           <p>
            And how about that scoring?
           </p>
           <p>
            The man known fondly as "The Glove" averaged 24.2 points per game during the 1999-00 season, and it was one of seven in which he broke past the 20-point barrier. He didn't do so in the most efficient fashion, but scoring was never supposed to be his forte.
           </p>
           <p>
            He was great at it, but defense was always the calling card. And it's always the first thing people will associate with him when discussing his ultimate legacy. Well, either that or his trash-talking, as ESPN.com's
            <a href="http://espn.go.com/nba/story/_/id/9636632/nba-there-never-another-gary-payton" target="_blank">
             J.A. Adande once highlighted
            </a>
            :
           </p>
           <blockquote>
            <p>
             Do a Google image search forGary Payton, and one of the earliest and most frequent pictures you'll see is of him
             <a href="http://a.espncdn.com/combiner/i?img=/photo/2013/0326/grant_g_glove_cr_640.jpg&amp;w=640&amp;h=360" target="new">
              jawing at Michael Jordan
             </a>
             in the middle of the 1996 NBA Finals. It looks like Payton is about to drop an F-bomb ... and according to his recollections, that's probably a safe bet.
            </p>
            <p>
             "It was a lot everything," Payton said. "A lot of 's---', 'f---', 'f--- you'. And thenRon Harpergot into it,Scottie Pippengot into it, Phil [Jackson] got into it. We were going back and forth with the 'f--- you.'"
            </p>
            <p>
             Sometimes the Google algorithms create an unfair or unfortunate link, capturing a moment that doesn't truly represent a person's life. This isn't one of those times. If the Basketball Hall of Fame had busts of its enshrinees like its pro football counterpart, Payton's would have to be cast with his head cocked and mouth open, perpetually talking trash.
            </p>
           </blockquote>
           <p>
            More often than not, he could back up the words.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             7. Isiah Thomas
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="7. Isiah Thomas" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/022/hi-res-b710ba27ddaf0aeee3c09141c1d9b999_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Andrew D. Bernstein/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1981-94
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Detroit Pistons
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 19.2 points, 3.6 rebounds, 9.3 assists, 1.9 steals, 0.3 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 22.9 points, 4.5 rebounds, 13.9 assists, 2.5 steals, 0.4 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/t/thomais01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 18.1 PER, .516 TS%, 106 ORtg, 107 DRtg, 80.7 WS, 0.109 WS/48, 0.318 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1921.41 PP, 549.79 AS, 176.47 CC, 13.57 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.2 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Somewhat unexpectedly, not all advanced stats and performance metrics care for Isiah Thomas.
           </p>
           <p>
            His career PER of 18.1 isn't exactly elite among his peers, and his win-share numbers are decidedly unimpressive. In fact, 24 studied point guards have earned more over the course of their careers, and 35 have earned them at a quicker rate.
           </p>
           <p>
            On top of that, Thomas earned just 13.57 Career Contributions per season, which beats out only Dennis Johnson, Mark Price, Rajon Rondo and Jo Jo White among ranked players.
           </p>
           <p>
            But don't worry. There are plenty of positives.
           </p>
           <p>
            Though he never made any All-Defensive squads, Thomas helped set the tone for the "Bad Boys" during the 1980s, even steering those Detroit Pistons to a pair of championships. He was a tough, gritty leader who thrived when he was doing the little things, such as playing on a severely sprained ankle and flat-out refusing to call it quits.
           </p>
           <p>
            Plus, context helps explain away some of the flaws in the resume. For example, of course he didn't earn too many Career Contributions when he was playing next to so many great players like Joe Dumars, Vinnie Johnson and Bill Laimbeer.
           </p>
           <p>
            All the lackluster metrics do push Thomas down in the rankings probably to a point that many will be surprised to find him appearing so soon, but it's not as though he wasn't a true all-time great at the 1.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             6. Bob Cousy
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="6. Bob Cousy" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/023/hi-res-e9fa35832ad89f0fd68281d7e7ae87ba_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Anonymous/Associated Press
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1950-70
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Boston Celtics, Cincinnati Royals
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 18.4 points, 5.2 rebounds, 7.5 assists
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 21.7 points, 6.9 rebounds, 9.5 assists
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/c/cousybo01.html">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 19.8 PER, .446 TS%, 91.1 WS, 0.139 WS/48, 0.882 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1620.83 PP, 1328.37 AS, 213.22 CC, 15.23 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            If there was any point guard who was ahead of his time, it was Bob Cousy.
           </p>
           <p>
            A true master of his position, Cousy wasn't content to play basketball the way it had always been played when he arrived in the league. He brought some flair to the hardwoodsome Houdini, if you willwith his behind-the-back passes, no-look endeavors and previously unseen levels of creativity. And not only did it dazzle crowds, but it helped the Boston Celtics win a lot of games, too.
           </p>
           <p>
            Sadly, assist percentage wasn't tracked until the final season of the floor general's career, and he only played seven games with the Cincinnati Royals during that time. But throughout that small sample, his assist percentage was a sparkling 32.0 while he averaged a career-high 10.6 dimes per 36 minutes.
           </p>
           <p>
            Chances are his actual career assist percentage would be lower, given his average of 7.9 assists per 36 minutes with the uptempo Celtics, but it would surely still be impressive.
           </p>
           <p>
            Cousy wasn't the most efficient scorer, but he still contributed enough on both ends to earn 15.23 Career Contributions per season while suiting up for 14 years. And since he played with so many Boston legends, that's even more of a standout number than it would typically be.
           </p>
           <p>
            Plus, the playoff metrics help out this 1-guard quite a bit. Though his Playoff Performance score leaves something to be desired, his 1,328.37 Advancement Shares trail only Magic Johnson at the position.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

              </span>
              Prev
             </a>
             <div class="paginators_counter">
              24
              <span class="br-light-grey">
               of
              </span>
              <span class="paginators_page-count">
               29
              </span>
             </div>
             <a class="paginator paginator--secondary paginator--next" href="javascript: void(0)" title="Next Page">
              Next
              <span class="aquo">
               
              </span>
             </a>
            </div>
            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             5. Walt Frazier
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="5. Walt Frazier" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/024/hi-res-2087830514181a8ad19a33f9cbe43705_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Associated Press
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1967-80
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : New York Knicks, Cleveland Cavaliers
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 18.9 points, 5.9 rebounds, 6.1 assists, 1.9 steals, 0.2 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 23.2 points, 7.3 rebounds, 8.2 assists, 2.4 steals, 0.3 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/f/fraziwa01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 19.1 PER, .542 TS%, 104 ORtg, 98 DRtg, 113.5 WS, 0.176 WS/48, 0.151 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1980.9 PP, 689.63 AS, 248.78 CC, 19.14 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 1.456 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            "
            <span>
             It's Clyde's ball," Willis Reed, Walt "Clyde" Frazier's teammate with the New York Knicks, once told
             <em>
              Sport
             </em>
             magazine (via
             <a href="http://www.nba.com/history/legends/walt-frazier/index.html">
              NBA.com
             </a>
             )."He just lets us play with it once in awhile."
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <span>
             I don't know if Reed meant it this way, but this statement applied to both ends of the court.
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <span>
             Frazier was a dynamic offensive player, one capable of scoring at will or distributing the rock out to his teammates with his deft passing skills. He was just as creative with the ball in his hands as he was fancy off the court, but he always seemed to find his targets once the ball left his hands.
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <span>
             But Frazier was even better at defense.
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <span>
             Arguably the greatest defensive point guard of all time, Frazier was a master of thievery, lulling the man he was guarding into a false sense of security before ripping the ball away and bursting down the court once he'd gained possession. He was named to seven All-Defensive teams during his career, and a Defensive Player of the Year selection wouldn't have been out of the question had the award existed back when he played.
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <span>
             Frazier was also remarkably valuable to his teams, many of which were quite successful during both the regular season and the playoffs. His 19.14 Career Contributions per season helped him earn 1.456 LMVP shares, more than any point guard in NBA history save Chris Paul and two of the players who have yet to show up in this countdown.
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <span>
             It's hard to determine which he was better atstanding out with his style, both on and off the court, or providing quality production to his teams on a nightly basis.
            </span>
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>
         <section class="article_page article_page--secondary js-article-page-25 cf display-none " data-page="25">
          <header class="article_page-header">
           <div class="article_nav-bar br-texture--sudo br-texture--sudo-fade-to-white">
            <div class="paginators paginators--top">
             <a class="paginator paginator--secondary paginator--prev" href="javascript: void(0)" title="Previous Page">
              <span class="aquo">
               
              </span>
              Prev
             </a>
             <div class="paginators_counter">

              <span class="br-light-grey">
               of
              </span>
              <span class="paginators_page-count">

              </span>
