            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             4. John Stockton
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="4. John Stockton" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/025/hi-res-5976bf4ccee4f5d735284a1438276e06_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Andrew D. Bernstein/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1984-2003
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Utah Jazz
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 13.1 points, 2.7 rebounds, 10.5 assists, 2.2 steals, 0.2 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 17.2 points, 3.3 rebounds, 14.5 assists, 3.2 steals, 0.3 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/s/stockjo01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 21.8 PER, .608 TS%, 121 ORtg, 104 DRtg, 207.7 WS, 0.209 WS/48, 0.161 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 2704.52 PP, 251.46 AS, 439.81 CC, 23.15 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 0.771 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            John Stockton was put in the perfect position to succeed.
           </p>
           <p>
            Not only was Jerry Sloan his head coach for much of his career, maximizing his talents on both ends of the court, but he played at a time when it was easier to earn assists (thanks, generous scorers!) and suited up next to Karl Malone. The power forward was the ideal pick-and-roll partner to use against a league that largely didn't know how to stop what wasat that timea novel system.
           </p>
           <p>
            Of course, plenty of players have had perfect situations thrown at them. Few have ever capitalized like Stockton did.
           </p>
           <p>
            Not only does he have the all-time assists record, which might as well be unbreakable, but he remains one of the best thieves the NBA has ever witnessed. And he was one of the best defensive point guards for that matter. Though Stockton was never a volume scorer, topping out at 17.2 points per game during the 1989-90 and 1990-91 seasons, and never thrived on the glass, he did just about everything else well.
           </p>
           <p>
            How about those 23.15 Career Contributions per season?
           </p>
           <p>
            That's the No. 4 mark at this position, and Stockton maintained his level of play for so long that he has the largest total Career Contributions figure of any point guard in NBA history. In fact, Kareem Abdul-Jabbar, Wilt Chamberlain, Adrian Dantley, Michael Jordan and Malone are the only players at any position above 439.81.
           </p>
           <p>
            The lone aspect of his resume holding him back was the frustrating inability to get over the hump. While Stockton's 2,704.52 Playoff Performance score trails only the top two players at his position, his Advancement Share, a mediocre 251.46, falls outside the top 20.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>
         <section class="article_page article_page--secondary js-article-page-26 cf display-none " data-page="26">
          <header class="article_page-header">
           <div class="article_nav-bar br-texture--sudo br-texture--sudo-fade-to-white">

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             3. Oscar Robertson
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="3. Oscar Robertson" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/026/hi-res-59fe531988a67e982a422b8373e86f62_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Dick Raphael/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1960-74
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Cincinnati Royals, Milwaukee Bucks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 25.7 points, 7.5 rebounds, 9.5 assists, 1.1 steals, 0.1 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 31.4 points, 12.5 rebounds, 11.5 assists, 1.1 steals, 0.1 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/r/roberos01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 23.2 PER, .564 TS%, 98 DRtg, 189.2 WS, 0.207 WS/48, 2.479 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 1928.98 PP, 407.64 AS, 437.5 CC, 31.25 CC/Season, 1 LMVP, 4.2 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Oscar Robertson's 1961-62 campaign with the Cincinnati Royals is one of the most famous in NBA history, seeing as he averaged 30.8 points, 12.5 rebounds and 11.4 assists per game to become the first and only player who has ever put up a season-long triple-double.
           </p>
           <p>
            But what often goes overlooked is that Robertson didn't just average a triple-double for one season. He might never have matched the feat during an isolated go-round, but here are his per-game numbers for the
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/r/roberos01.html#1961-1966-sum:per_game">
             first six seasons
            </a>
            of his career combined:
           </p>
           <p>
            30.4 points. 10.0 rebounds. 10.7 assists.
           </p>
           <p>
            That's a triple-double right there.
           </p>
           <p>
            Robertson's all-around excellence allowed him to earn quite a few win shares, as you might expect. In addition to a studly 23.2 PER, he accumulated 189.2 win shares over the course of his career, more than any point guard other than John Stockton. They piled up at a rate of 0.207 per 48 minutes, faster than all but four floor generals throughout NBA history.
           </p>
           <p>
            But it's in the performance metrics where Robertson stands out, even if his Advancement Shares aren't too special and are boosted by Kareem Abdul-Jabbar's presence when they joined forces on the Milwaukee Bucks.
           </p>
           <p>
            Robertson's 31.25 Career Contributions per season are not only the top mark at the position, but they blow everyone else out. Chris Paul's 28.6 are the No. 2 average, and only the next player to appear in this countdown joins them in the above-25 club. Just six players at any position top Robertson here, in fact.
           </p>
           <p>
            He's also one of only three point guards to win LMVP, his 2.479 MVP Shares trail only the top-ranked player at the position and his 4.2 LMVP Shares place him behind just seven players in NBA history, none of whom are point guards.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

          <header class="article_page-header">
           <div class="article_nav-bar br-texture--sudo br-texture--sudo-fade-to-white">

              <span class="br-light-grey">

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             2. Jerry West
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="2. Jerry West" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/027/hi-res-91389fdf38670a2cc45038eae40eb99e_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Dick Raphael/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1960-74
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Los Angeles Lakers
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 27.0 points, 5.8 rebounds, 6.7 assists, 2.6 steals, 0.7 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 31.3 points, 7.9 rebounds, 9.7 assists, 2.6 steals, 0.7 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/w/westje01.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 22.9 PER, .550 TS%, 95 DRtg, 162.6 WS, 0.213 WS/48, 2.090 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 3489.93 PP, 955.83 AS, 356.81 CC, 25.49 CC/Season, 1 LMVP, 2.687 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            Yes, Jerry West qualifies as a point guard.
           </p>
           <p>
            As stated multiple times throughout this article and the historical series as a whole, positional designations were not made subjectively, but rather determined by whichever position
            <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/w/westje01.html">
             Basketball-Reference.com
            </a>
            lists the player at more frequently throughout the course of his career.
           </p>
           <p>
            While this might be surprising, given that West is typically found in rankings of the league's premier 2-guards, he was listed as a point guard during 12 of his career's 14 seasons.
           </p>
           <p>
            So, point guard it is.
           </p>
           <p>
            In 1972,
            <em>
             Sports Illustrated
            </em>
            ran a piece with the following quote, as Bill Simmons relayed in
            <em>
             The Book of Basketba
            </em>
            <em>
             ll
            </em>
            : "There has been a groundswell for West the last few seasons, so that now he is often accepted as the equal, or the superior, of Oscar Robertson as the finest guard of all time."
           </p>
           <p>
            Obviously, players have come around and dethroned those two, but it was a valid sentiment at the time. Plus, West only built his resume with two more deserving All-Star seasons, one of which saw him named First Team All-NBA. Meanwhile, Robertson quickly declined during the years just following that
            <em>
             Sports Illustrated
            </em>
            claim.
            <em>
             <br/>
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            Throughout their careers as a whole, the two point guards had fairly similar resumes. Robertson was a slightly better offensive player in a vacuum, and he was certainly more valuable to his teams, given West's superstar teammates. But the man who would go on to be the model for the NBA's logo was an unbelievably gifted defender, which is part of what pushes him over the top.
           </p>
           <p>
            So too does his playoff success, as despite only winning a single ring, West consistently put his team in a position to take home the trophy. Even without too many titles working in his favor, he finishes No. 2 and No. 6 in Playoff Performance and Advancement Shares respectively among point guards.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>

            <h2 class="article_page-title" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
             1. Magic Johnson
            </h2>
           </div>
          </header>
          <div class="article_image" itemprop="associatedMedia">
           <div class="article_image--main " itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" style="max-width:630px">
            <img alt="1. Magic Johnson" data-defer-src="http://img.bleacherreport.net/img/slides/photos/003/798/028/hi-res-60de377a10943230e781f318282960f6_crop_north.jpg?w=630&amp;h=420&amp;q=75" height="420" itemprop="contentURL" src="data:image/png;base64,iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAAAEAAAABCAQAAAC1HAwCAAAAC0lEQVQIHWNgYAAAAAMAAU9ICq8AAAAASUVORK5CYII=" width="630"/>
            <div class="caption-overlay border-box" max-width="630">
            </div>
            <div class="article_image-accreditation" max-width="630">
             <span>
              Andrew D. Bernstein/Getty Images
             </span>
             <br/>
            </div>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="article_body cf" itemprop="articleBody">
           <p>
            <strong>
             Years Played
            </strong>
            : 1979-96
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Teams
            </strong>
            : Los Angeles Lakers
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career Per-Game Stats
            </strong>
            : 19.5 points, 7.2 rebounds, 11.2 assists, 1.9 steals, 0.4 blocks
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Ultimate Season
            </strong>
            <span>
             : 23.9 points, 9.6 rebounds, 13.1 assists, 3.4 steals, 0.7 blocks
            </span>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Career
             <a href="http://www.basketball-reference.com/players/j/johnsma02.html#advanced::none">
              Advanced Stats
             </a>
            </strong>
            : 24.1 PER, .610 TS%, 121 ORtg, 104 DRtg, 155.8 WS, 0.225 WS/48, 5.129 MVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             Performance Metrics
            </strong>
            : 4208.5 PP, 1384.98 AS, 270.76 CC, 20.83 CC/Season, 0 LMVPs, 1.2 LMVP Shares
           </p>
           <p>
           </p>
           <p>
            The only flaw on Magic Johnson's resume is the lack of an LMVP, which is rather hard to earn when you're consistently playing on teams that are right near the top of the NBA standings year in and year out.
           </p>
           <p>
            Other than that, he has it all covered.
           </p>
           <p>
            Just take a peek at all the categories in which he leads the field of point guards: Playoff Performance, Advancement Shares, assists per game, true shooting percentage (even without a potent perimeter jumper), total rebounding percentage, MVPs and MVP shares.
           </p>
           <p>
            Beyond that, he finishes in the top five for Career Contributions per season, titles, rebounds per game, blocks per game, PER, offensive rating, win shares, win shares per 48 minutes, All-Star selections and All-NBA honors.
           </p>
           <p>
            But perhaps most impressively, he put together this type of legacy despite having his career cut short.
           </p>
           <p>
            Johnson missed four seasons in his early 30s as he dealt with HIV. And though he was still a valuable player when he returned to the Los Angeles Lakers for the 1995-96 campaign, he clearly wasn't the legendary floor general he'd previously been.
           </p>
           <p>
            Whether we're talking about scoring, distributing, rebounding, providing value to his teams, playing efficient basketball, making crowds believe in, well, Magic, winning playoff games or doing whatever it took to win, this particular 1-guard was the best in the business.
           </p>
           <hr/>
           <p>
            <em>
             <strong>
              Metric Cheat Sheet
             </strong>
             (refer to the second slide for more detailed descriptions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             PP = Playoff Performance (individual success and longevity in the playoffs)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             AS = Advancement Shares (team postseason success weighted by importance of contributions)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             CC = Career Contributions (scaled importance to team with team quality factored in)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP = Literal MVP (earned with highest Career Contributions score in the league)
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             LMVP Shares = Literal MVP Shares (gives credit to non-first-place finishers in the LMVP standings each year)
            </em>
           </p>
          </div>
         </section>
        </div>

    // Load the SDK asynchronously
    (function(){
        // If we've already installed the SDK, we're done
        if (document.getElementById('facebook-jssdk')) {return;}

        // Get the first script element, which we'll use to find the parent node
        var firstScriptElement = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];

        // Create a new script element and set its id
        var facebookJS = document.createElement('script');
        facebookJS.id = 'facebook-jssdk';

        // Set the new script's source to the source of the Facebook JS SDK

        // Insert the Facebook JS SDK into the DOM
